Or I could turn, and walk back toward the wide, clean canals, to the office with the dry heat and the taste of nothing, to the life that had been given to me, where I would be a useful, silent cog, turning in the dark, telling the stories I was fed until I forgot I had a voice of my own.

The mist beaded on the wool of my government-issue coat. I looked at my reflection in the tavern window—a blurred, grey shape against the glow of human noise.

Slowly, I unbuttoned the coat. I took it off. I folded it over my arm, hiding the institutional grey. Beneath, I wore a simple, dark dress. It was my own.

I pushed open the door of *De Visser*.

The warmth was a physical blow. The smell hit me—stale beer, brine, the tang of worked bodies—like a forgotten language. The low murmur stumbled, then died. Heads turned. Eyes, hard and curious, appraised the woman who walked in alone. They saw the dress, the face that was no longer a girl’s, the eyes that had seen the bottom of things.

I met the glances. I held them. I did not look down. I did not smile.

I walked to the zinc counter. Jan, older, greyer, but still built like a barrel, looked up from a glass he was polishing. His eyes took me in. They flickered over the folded coat on my arm. They saw the woman beneath it. He gave no sign of recognition. No sign of welcome.

He placed the polished glass down. He reached for the bottle of *jenever*. He poured two fingers into a chipped tumbler and pushed it across the wood.

I laid the single, worn guilden on the counter between us. It was not enough. It was never enough.

He looked at the coin. He looked at me. His gaze was flat, unreadable as the canal water outside.

Then, with a slow, deliberate motion, he pushed the coin back toward me. He gave the faintest shake of his head. His eyes held mine, and in their depths was an older, simpler understanding. Payment was expected later.

In coin, or in kind.

I took the glass. My fingers closed around the chill of it. I did not drink. I turned, leaning my back against the solid wood of the bar, facing the room. I looked at the men hunched over their drinks, at the shadows pooled in the corners, at the world that was real and rough and unforgiving.

I had come back to the beginning. But I was not the same. The stray cat was gone. In its place was something harder to define. A woman who knew the price of the machine, and the cost of staying out of it. A woman with a story she would never tell, and a cold inside her that no warmth could touch.

But she was here. In her own dress. With a coin in her pocket that was her own. And a glass in her hand, paid for on a promise.

The murmur of conversation started up again, lower now, weaving around me. I was part of the scenery again. A fixture. I took a sip. The *jenever* burned its familiar, cleansing path down my throat.

The door opened again, letting in a slice of damp, grey light. A man entered, shaking water from his hat. He was young, his face drawn with a worry too sharp for his years. He scanned the room, his eyes skipping over the regulars, searching. They landed on me. They hesitated. A woman, alone at the bar.

He approached. Not with the swagger of the regulars, but with the tentative step of someone seeking something specific in the wrong place.

“Mevrouw?” he said, his voice barely above the rumble of the room. “They said… they said I might find someone here. Someone who asks questions.”

I turned my head slowly. I looked at him. I said nothing. I just waited, the glass cool in my hand.

He swallowed, his Adam’s apple bobbing. “It’s my sister. She’s gone. The police, they say she’s just run off. But she wouldn’t. Not like this.” He reached into his inner pocket, pulled out a folded, much-handled piece of paper. A cheap lithograph. A girl’s face, smiling stiffly. “They won’t listen. They have their story.”

I looked from the paper to his eyes. I saw the desperation there. The refusal to be digested by the easy narrative. The grit in the machine.

The cold inside me shifted. It did not thaw. It focused. It became a point, a needle of ice.

I looked past him, to Jan behind the bar. He was watching, his expression unchanged. I raised my glass a fraction of an inch. An acknowledgment. A promise.

I turned back to the young man. I took the lithograph from his trembling fingers. The paper was cheap. The girl’s smile was a lie. But the question in her brother’s eyes was the truest thing I had seen all day.

“Sit down,” I said, my voice rough with the *jenever* and the long silence of official rooms. I nodded to the stool beside me. “Tell me.”

He sat. The machine turned in its distant, well-oiled chambers. But here, in the warm, smelling dark of *De Visser*, a different kind of work was beginning. A smaller grind. A human one.

I took another sip, and listened.
